^ H E Council desire to acknowledge their
indebtedness for the general preparation
of the Catalogue to Mrs. V. V. Farone,
Mr. Stanley De Brath, Hon. Librarian, and
Captain A. A. Carnell.
The classification was arranged by Mrs. Farone.
The Glossary of Terms was compiled by
Mr. De Brath. On his advice a number of
books have been withdrawn as unsuitable.
Others are marked “ OBS ” to signify that
they are obsolete. These are retained for
their historical interest.
RULES OF THE LIBRARY
1. Members may have three books at one time, of which only one
may be “new.”
2. Books are reckoned as “ new ” for twelve months after publication.
3. Books must be returned within fourteen days from date of issue.
The non-observance of this rule has given rise to so many complaints
and to so much inconvenience to other readers that the Committee
regret to have to re-introduce the fine of 2d. per week per volume
for books not returned to date. Renewal of issue for a second
fortnight can be applied for by post card. If the books are not
required by another Member, this permission can be assumed.
4. If Members repeatedly retain books after special application has
been made for them, there will be the regrettable necessity of refusing
such Members the further use of the Library.
5. Any Member bespeaking a special book not immediately available
can have it forwarded later by sending or leaving 4d. with the
Librarian to cover the cost of postage, or a post card will be sent
when the book is available.
6. A uniform charge of 9d. is made for each parcel of books, which
amount must be forwarded preferably in stamps, each time a request
for books is made. Parcels must be returned carriage paid.
7. Borrowers are requested to keep the books clean, and on no account
to turn down the leaves or to disfigure thepages bj pencil or other marks.
They are also specially asked to see that the books are well wrapped
with a thick inside lajer o fpaper to protect them from injury in transit
to the library, as they will be held responsible for any damage which
might with ordinary care have been prevented.
Hours : 10—6 p.m. on week-days. Saturdays 1 p.m. Thursdays
during Session until commencement of lectures.
IMPORTANT NOTICE.
Members occasionally lend Library books to friends. While this is
recognised as excellent propaganda, discretion is requested with regard
to new and popular books in constant demand by Members.
In such cases Members are asked to remember their responsibility
in returning books to time.
Glossary of Terms Used in Books on
Psychic Study
A gent. The active person who seems to originate a telepathic transmission.
Amnesia. Loss of memory.
A nimism, (i ) In ancient religions, that every object has a soul,
(2) Now used for the theory that all supernormal phenomena are
produced by an incarnate mind.
A pport. The production of objects brought from a distance supernormally.
Asport. The supernormal disappearance of any object.
Astral, (i ) A Theosophical term used of the plane of being above the physical
and below the Mental. (2) in Spiritualism anything not of this world.
A ora. An emanation surrounding the body, visible to some clairvoyants.
Autognosis. Knowledge of the internal condition and appearance of any
part of the body, usually in disease.
A utomatic W riting, drawing, painting, etc. Automatism (q.v.) taking
these forms.
A utomatism. An act produced independently of the normal will.
A utoscopy. Vision of the interior of one’s own body, clairvoyantly or under
hypnosis.
Auto-Suggestion. Self-suggestion.
Clairaudience. The internal perception of sounds not normally audible.
Clairvoyance. The perception of things not normally visible, whether by
reason of distance in space or time, or by being in closed envelopes.
Control. Used of the Intelligence which purports to communicate messages
written or uttered by the automatist, sensitive or medium.
Cross-Correspondence. Messages received by two or more automatists,
independently of each other, and only fully intelligible when taken in con­
nection by an unrelated person.
Cryptomnesia. The hidden memory, seemingly perfect, which reproduces
words or acts long past.
Cryptesthesia. A hidden faculty covering all phenomena of perception.
(Originated by Richet, see “ Thirty Years of Psychical Research,” p. 64).
Cryftoscopy. See Clairvoyance.
Direct Voice. A voice produced without mechanical or human action, but
in the presence of a medium.
D irect W ritin g . Writing produced in the same way.
Dynamo-Psychism. A soul-energy ; both universally in theworld and resident in
the individual. The intermediate betwe-nMatterand Spirit. See “Perisprit.”
Ectoplasm. The material emanation from the body of a physical medium.
Endoscopy. A synonym for autoscopy.
Etheric Double. Used by Theosophists for the “ subtle body ” or invisible
body of ether, inseparable, except by death, from the physical; often used
by Spiritualists for the life-like appearance of a person in a different place
from the body.
Extra (Photographic). Any appearance on a photograph (usually a face)
which cannot be objectively accounted for.
Hetero-Suggestion. Suggestion from outside of the normal consciousness.
Hyper-Esthesia. A metapsychic exaltation of the bodily senses.
Hypnagogic. The sights and sounds, usually vivid, which accompany the
approach of sleep. (Originated by A. Maury, and used by F. W. H. Myers).
Hypnopompic. Dream images lasting into the waking state.
Ideoplasty. The power of ideas to control the form of matter, used with
reference to materialisation. “ Ideoplasm ” used sometimes as a synonym
for ectoplasm.
Levitation. A raising of objects from the ground by supposed supernormal
means, but especially of living persons. (Myers’ def. Hum. Pers. Vol. I.,
Ch. xviii).
Materialisation. A word used by Spiritualists to denote the unknown process
by which a spirit is deemed to be able to mould ectoplasm to become tangible
and visible.
Medium. A person of peculiar constitution through whom communication
is made with the discamate. Also used for those in whose presence super­
normal phenomena occur.
Metagnomy. The term is used by Dr. Eugene Osty to cover all the activities
which we understand by mediumship, especially mental forms.
Metapsychics. The science of facts which are not explicable by official psycho­
logy. “ Metapsychic facts are marked off from the physical in that they
seem due to an unknown intelligence whether human or non-human.”
(Richet).
Obsession. The tyranny exercised over a living person by one in the unseen.
Para-Psychic. See Metapsychics.
Percipient. The person affected by an experience.
Perisprit. The French term for the individualised dynamo-psychism through
which they consider that the spirit of a man acts (otherwise the “ astral
body ” or double).
Personality. An individual, assuming that his distinguishing traits are the
mask (persona) of the real Self—The Spirit or living soul. Also used
less properly, for ‘ individuality.’
Poltergeist. A kind of haunting characterised by the movement of loose
objects, •accompanied by breakages and sometimes by injury to people.
Always confined to a particular place and the neighbourhood of a particular
person.
Pneumatography. Literally, spirit writing. It may mean “ Direct ” writing
or Automatic writing.
Promnesia, more often Paramnesia. False memory; the sense that a thing
has happened before, deja vu.
Prosopopesis. The assumption of a mask; hence, the term used by animists
for the “ fictitious personalities ” induced by auto-suggestion or hetero­
suggestion.
Psyche, psychic. The soul, having to do with the soul of man ; now used to
mean a medium, or anything relating to metapsychics, “ A psychic book
or person.”
Psychiatry. Mental hygiene, medical psychological treatment o f the insane.
Psychography. Writing supernormally produced on photographic plates.
Psychometry. The faculty of perceiving the nature, surroundings or other
characters of a material object by meta-psychic means.
Psychorragy. Power of producing phantoms, or projecting the Double.
Psychotherapy. Mental or spiritual healing.
Retrocognition. Knowledge of the past, supernormally obtained.
Sensitive. Used as a noun, means a medium who is open to impressions which
may or may not be due to discamates.
Skotography. Appearances on a photographic plate kept in complete darkness,
but in the presence of a medium.
Slate W riting. Writing supernormally produced on a slate, or slates.
Soul. The supposed etherial body animated by the same spirit or mind o f a
person. “ May your spirit and soul, and body be preserved entire ” ( i.
Thess. V. 23). Used in Scripture for “ the Life ” ; by spiritualists for
the psyche.
Spirit. The individualised Mind, independently of the body and the soul;
the essential element of individuality; the life of the world ; a discarnate
being.
Spirit-Photography. A misnomer for supernormal photography.
Subconscious. Every element, past or present, not included in the waking
consciousness at any given moment.
Subliminal. That which lies belowthe threshold (limen) of human conscious­
ness, as opposed to the Supra-liminal lying above that threshold.
Telekinesis. - Action at a distance, the movement of objects without apparent
contact.
Telepathy. The communication of impressions of any kind from one mind to
another independently of the normal channels of sense.
Teleplasm. Another name for ectoplasm.
Telesthesia. See Cryptesthesia.
T rance. A condition of unconsciousness in a medium or a hypnotised person.
V eridical. Truth-speaking, used of hallucinations which correspond to real
events happening elsewhere and unknown to the percipient.

The Attitude of the
London Spiritualist Alliance
towards Spiritualism & Psychical
Research
There is one belief common to all spiritualists,
no matter how widely they may differ con­
cerning the various issues involved, that is,
a belief in the Survival of Human Personality
after Bodily Death and its demonstration.
The Alliance regards Human Survival as a
fact in Nature.
It recognises that the inferences arising from
this fact are scientific, philosophical and
religious.
It affirms that psychical research should
be encouraged, but deprecates unscientific
and aimless pursuit of phenomena as an end
in itself.
It looks upon psychic phenomena as the
“ preamble to all religions ” (F. W. H. Myers)
and not in themselves the basis for a New
Religion.
It realises that while some psychic phenomena
emanate from the discarnate, others do not.
It recommends that every communication be
subjected to critical examination.
